Applying the Conditions to the Vision of God and the Initial Response
Proof that vision of God is necessary if two conditions apply, and the refutation of that proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the presence of conditions for vision does not necessitate the vision of God, and how the example of a large object seen as small from a distance refutes that necessity.

Core Idea
Conditions do not guarantee vision; visible experience proves otherwise.

This passage presents a theological argument about whether the vision of God is necessary when certain conditions are met. The opponent claims that since two conditions (soundness of sense and vision) apply to God and are present now, vision must occur. The response cleverly challenges this by pointing to a common visual experience: a large object seen from a distance appears small, meaning some parts are unseen despite equal conditions. This lesson unpacks that argument and the subsequent geometric objection and its rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage along with the English translation to get an overall sense of the argument.
Pay attention to the logical flow from proof to response to objection to final rebuttal.
2
Identify the opponent's proof and the first response
Find where the opponent argues that vision is necessary because two conditions are present. Then locate the first response: 'We do not concede necessity' and the example of a large object seen as small.
The example shows that conditions can be present without full vision.
3
Examine the geometric objection and its refutation
Understand the objection about lines from the eye forming a triangle, making parts unequal in distance. Then see how the refutation argues that if distance were the cause, the object would not be seen at all.
The refutation uses a 'cubit' assumption to show distance is irrelevant.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نعقل من هذه الشرائط في حق رؤية الله تعالى إلا سلامة الحاسة وصحة الرؤية لكون) الست ( البواقي ( منها ( مختصة بالأجسام وهما أي الشرطان المعقولان في رؤيته حاصلان الآن فوجب حصول رؤيته . ) والجواب : ( عن هذه الشبهة إما أولاً : فهو ( أنا لا نسلم وجوب الرؤية عند اجتماع الشروط الثمانية ) وذلك لأن دليلكم وإن دل عليه لكن عندنا ما ينفيه ( لأنا نرى الجسم الكبير من البعيد صغيراً وما ذلك إلا لأنا نرى بعض أجزائه دون البعض مع تساوي الكل في حصول الشرائط ) . فظهر أنه لا تجب الرؤية عند اجتماعها ( لا يقال : يتصل بطرفي المرئي من العين خطان شعاعيان كساقي مثلث قاعدته سطح المرئي ويخرج منها ) أي من العين (إلى وسطه خط قائم عليه ) أي على سطحه ( يقسم ) ذلك الخط ( المثلث ) المذكور (إلى مثلثين قائمتين (الزاوية الواقعة عن جنبتي الخط القائم ( فيكون ) الخط الوسط ( وتراً لكل واحدة من الزاويتين الحادتين وكل من الطرفين وتر الزاوية قائمة ووتر القائمة ) في المثلث ( أطول من وتر الحادة فلم تكن أجزاء المرئي متساوية في القرب والبعد ) بالنسبة إلى الرائي بل يكون وسط المرئي أقرب إليه من طرفيه، فجاز أن يرى الوسط وحده بدون الطرفين ( لأنا نقول : نفرض هذا التفاوت الذي ذكرتموه في هذه ) الخطوط ) ذراعاً فلو كان عدم رؤية الطرفين لأجل البعد فإذا فرض أنه ( بعد المرئي يقدر ذلك ( البعد الذي لطرفيه ) وجب أن لا يرى أصلاً ، وإذا يرى فهذا البعد لا أثر له في عدم الرؤية ) . فتكون الأجزاء كلها مع ذلك التفاوت متساوية في حصول شرائط الرؤية، وبعضها غير مرئي فلا تجب الرؤية مع حصولها ) .
We understand from these conditions with respect to the vision of God Almighty only soundness of the sense and soundness of vision, because the remaining six are specific to bodies. And these two—i.e., the two conditions understood in His vision—are present now, so His vision must occur. And the answer to this doubt: First, we do not concede the necessity of vision when the eight conditions are met, because your proof indicates it, but we have something that negates it: we see the large body from a distance as small, and that is only because we see some of its parts without others, while all are equal in the obtaining of the conditions. So it appears that vision is not necessary when they are met. It cannot be said: Two ray-like lines connect from the eye to the two ends of the seen object like the legs of a triangle whose base is the surface of the seen object, and a line emerges from the eye to its center perpendicular to its surface, dividing that triangle into two right triangles with the right angles on either side of the perpendicular line, so the middle line is the hypotenuse for each of the two acute angles, and each of the ends is the hypotenuse of a right angle, and the hypotenuse of a right angle in a triangle is longer than the hypotenuse of an acute angle, so the parts of the seen object are not equal in closeness and distance relative to the seer, but rather the center is closer than the ends, so it is possible that the center alone is seen without the ends. For we say: We assume this difference you mentioned in these lines to be a cubit. If the non-vision of the ends were due to distance, then if it is assumed that the distance of the seen object is equal to that distance of its ends, then it would be necessary that it not be seen at all. But since it is seen, this distance has no effect on non-vision. So all the parts, despite that difference, are equal in the obtaining of the conditions of vision, and some of them are not visible, so vision is not necessary when they are obtained.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage presents the argument that from the eight conditions, only two (soundness of sense and soundness of vision) are applicable to God, and since they are present now, vision of God must occur. The answer is that we do not concede that vision is necessary when the eight conditions are met, because we see a large object from a distance as small, meaning we see some parts but not others, so conditions can be present without full vision. Then an objection is raised: lines from the eye to the object create a triangle where the edges are farther, so parts are not equally close. The response is that even if we assume a difference of a cubit, if the object were that far, it would not be seen at all, so distance does not affect non-vision. Thus, parts are equal in conditions yet some are not seen, so vision is not necessary when conditions are met.

Misconception: If all conditions for vision are met, vision must necessarily occur.
Correction: The example of a large object seen as small shows that conditions can be met without full vision; thus necessity is not proven.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The conditions for vision do not necessitate vision; the example of a distant large object shows that conditions can be present without complete visibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The example of the distant object shows that even when all conditions are equal, some parts remain unseen. In your spiritual life, are there aspects of God's signs or attributes that you miss because of your own perspective or assumptions? What would it take for you to 'adjust your line of sight' to see Him more clearly?
?
If the opponent's claim (that vision must occur now) were true, you would be required to see God in this life. Do you realize that your inability to see Him, despite your faith and spiritual striving, is actually a mercy—preventing a false necessity? How does that change your relationship with the unseen?

The Objection of Sahib al-Lubab
Objection to the previous response regarding seeing all parts and size perception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that seeing all parts of an object does not necessarily mean seeing it as large, and how the angle of vision can affect perceived size.

Core Idea
Seeing all parts does not guarantee large size; angle matters.

This lesson presents an objection from Sahib al-Lubab against the previous argument that seeing all parts implies seeing the object as large. The objection introduces the concept of the cone of vision and how a narrow angle can make something appear smaller even if all parts are seen.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic and translation of the objection carefully. Notice how it challenges the previous response.
2
Identify the key claim
Identify the main claim: that seeing all parts does not entail seeing large because size can depend on the angle.
The objection uses the concept of 'ضيق الزاوية' (narrowness of angle).
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this objection opens up a new dimension in the discussion about vision of God.
This is a subtle point about perception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال بعض الفضلاء ) أي صاحب اللباب معترضا على هذه المعارضة : ( لا يلزم من رؤيتنا جميع أجزائه أن نراه كبيراً ، وإنما يلزم ذلك أن لو كانت رؤيته صغيراً وكبيرا بحسب رؤية الأجزاء وعدمها وهو ممنوع ( فلعل رؤيته صغيراً وكبيراً تختلف بضيق الزاوية الحاصلة في الناظر من قوله : (مع أنه يمكن إدراجه في حضوره للحاسة ) إدراجه فيه مع عدم اندراج شيء من باقي الشروط فيه سيما عدم غاية البعد لا يخلو عن تمحل كما لا يخفى. قوله: ( وصحة الرؤية) أي مع حضوره للحاسة بالتفسير الذي سبق، إذ قد عرفت أن المعدود من الشرائط الثمانية ليس مطلق صحة الرؤية . قوله : (حاصلان الآن فيه منع لأن معنى السلامة حصول قوة مشروطة في الإبصار فلم لا يجوز أن لا يحصل قوة مشروطة في إدراكه تعالى . قوله : (لأنا نقول : نفرض إلخ) وأيضاً نفرض سطح المرئي منحنياً بحيث يكون طرفاه أقرب من وسطه بقدر التفاوت المذكور كشكل نصف قالب إسطوانة أو شكل مجن تقعره إلى محاذاة الرائي، وحينئذ يجب أن يرى أصغر مع أنه خلاف الواقع.
Some virtuous person, i.e., the author of Lubab, objected to this rebuttal: "It does not follow from our seeing all its parts that we see it large. Rather, that would follow if its being seen small or large were according to the seeing of parts and their non-seeing, which is denied. Perhaps its being seen small or large differs according to the narrowness of the angle occurring in the viewer from." Then his statement: (With that it can be included in its presence to the sense) — its inclusion therein, while nothing of the remaining conditions is included in it, especially absence of extreme distance, is not free from forcedness, as is not hidden. His statement: (And soundness of vision) i.e., with its presence to the sense according to the preceding interpretation, for you have known that what is counted among the eight conditions is not absolute soundness of vision. His statement: (They are present now) — in it is a denial, because the meaning of soundness is the obtaining of a strength conditioned in sight. So why may it not be that a strength conditioned in perceiving Him, may He be exalted, is not obtained? His statement: (For we say: We assume, etc.) — and also we assume the surface of the seen object to be curved such that its ends are closer than its center by the amount of the mentioned difference, like the shape of half a cylinder or the shape of a bow with its concavity facing the viewer, and then it would necessarily be seen smaller, contrary to reality.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage contains an objection from the author of al-Lubab against the previous response: seeing all parts does not entail seeing the object as large, because size could depend on the angle of the cone of vision. Then there are marginal comments: first, including the ninth condition (being luminous) in the second condition is forced; second, 'soundness of vision' here means with presence to the sense as previously interpreted; third, the statement that the two conditions are present now is disputed because soundness may require a specific strength; fourth, an additional response to the geometric argument: assume the surface is concave such that the edges are closer than the center, then it would be seen as smaller, contrary to reality. These clarifications address the earlier arguments.

Key Terms
صاحب اللباب
الزاوية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if you see all parts of an object, it must appear large.
Correction: Seeing all parts does not guarantee large size because perspective and viewing angle can make the same object appear small.

Checkpoint
According to Sahib al-Lubab's objection, what determines whether an object is seen as small or large?
A) The number of parts seen
B) The narrowness of the angle in the viewer
C) The absolute size of the object
D) The presence of the object to the sense

Key Takeaway
Seeing all parts does not entail seeing large; the angle of vision can affect perceived size.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Margins on Inclusion and Soundness
Marginal comments on the forced inclusion of a condition and the interpretation of soundness of vision.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two marginal clarifications: the forcedness of including the ninth condition in the second, and the correct interpretation of 'soundness of vision'.

Core Idea
Conditions must be properly defined and not forced into categories.

The marginal notes here address two points: first, the attempted inclusion of the ninth condition (being luminous) into the second condition (presence to the sense) is considered forced and problematic. Second, the phrase 'soundness of vision' is clarified to mean presence to the sense in the specific interpretation given earlier, not absolute soundness.

Guided Path
1
Read the first margin
Read the comment about including the ninth condition. Notice the criticism of forcedness (تمحل).
تمحل means forced interpretation.
2
Read the second margin
Read the comment about soundness of vision. Understand that it is not absolute soundness but a specific meaning.
The phrase 'صحة الرؤية' is reinterpreted.
3
Connect to the main argument
Consider how these clarifications refine the earlier discussion about conditions for vision.

Margins on Conditions and Geometry
Marginal comments on the condition of soundness and a geometric argument about curvature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two further marginal points: a denial that the conditions are present now, and a geometric argument about a concave surface affecting perceived size.

Core Idea
Conditions might not be met, and geometry can affect perception.

The final marginal comments address two issues: first, a denial that the conditions (soundness of vision and presence) are present, because soundness requires a specific strength that may not apply to perceiving God. Second, a geometric argument: if the object's surface is concave, the edges are closer to the viewer than the center, causing the object to appear smaller, which contradicts reality.

Guided Path
1
Read the third margin
Read the comment about the conditions not being present now. Note the argument about 'قوة مشروطة' (conditioned strength).
2
Read the fourth margin
Read the geometric argument about a curved surface. Understand how it implies seeing smaller.
This is a rebuttal to a previous geometric point.
3
Synthesize
Consider how these marginal comments serve to refine and defend the overall argument about vision.
They show the complexity of the discussion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال بعض الفضلاء ) أي صاحب اللباب معترضا على هذه المعارضة : ( لا يلزم من رؤيتنا جميع أجزائه أن نراه كبيراً ، وإنما يلزم ذلك أن لو كانت رؤيته صغيراً وكبيرا بحسب رؤية الأجزاء وعدمها وهو ممنوع ( فلعل رؤيته صغيراً وكبيراً تختلف بضيق الزاوية الحاصلة في الناظر من قوله : (مع أنه يمكن إدراجه في حضوره للحاسة ) إدراجه فيه مع عدم اندراج شيء من باقي الشروط فيه سيما عدم غاية البعد لا يخلو عن تمحل كما لا يخفى. قوله: ( وصحة الرؤية) أي مع حضوره للحاسة بالتفسير الذي سبق، إذ قد عرفت أن المعدود من الشرائط الثمانية ليس مطلق صحة الرؤية . قوله : (حاصلان الآن فيه منع لأن معنى السلامة حصول قوة مشروطة في الإبصار فلم لا يجوز أن لا يحصل قوة مشروطة في إدراكه تعالى . قوله : (لأنا نقول : نفرض إلخ) وأيضاً نفرض سطح المرئي منحنياً بحيث يكون طرفاه أقرب من وسطه بقدر التفاوت المذكور كشكل نصف قالب إسطوانة أو شكل مجن تقعره إلى محاذاة الرائي، وحينئذ يجب أن يرى أصغر مع أنه خلاف الواقع.
Some virtuous person, i.e., the author of Lubab, objected to this rebuttal: "It does not follow from our seeing all its parts that we see it large. Rather, that would follow if its being seen small or large were according to the seeing of parts and their non-seeing, which is denied. Perhaps its being seen small or large differs according to the narrowness of the angle occurring in the viewer from." Then his statement: (With that it can be included in its presence to the sense) — its inclusion therein, while nothing of the remaining conditions is included in it, especially absence of extreme distance, is not free from forcedness, as is not hidden. His statement: (And soundness of vision) i.e., with its presence to the sense according to the preceding interpretation, for you have known that what is counted among the eight conditions is not absolute soundness of vision. His statement: (They are present now) — in it is a denial, because the meaning of soundness is the obtaining of a strength conditioned in sight. So why may it not be that a strength conditioned in perceiving Him, may He be exalted, is not obtained? His statement: (For we say: We assume, etc.) — and also we assume the surface of the seen object to be curved such that its ends are closer than its center by the amount of the mentioned difference, like the shape of half a cylinder or the shape of a bow with its concavity facing the viewer, and then it would necessarily be seen smaller, contrary to reality.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage contains an objection from the author of al-Lubab against the previous response: seeing all parts does not entail seeing the object as large, because size could depend on the angle of the cone of vision. Then there are marginal comments: first, including the ninth condition (being luminous) in the second condition is forced; second, 'soundness of vision' here means with presence to the sense as previously interpreted; third, the statement that the two conditions are present now is disputed because soundness may require a specific strength; fourth, an additional response to the geometric argument: assume the surface is concave such that the edges are closer than the center, then it would be seen as smaller, contrary to reality. These clarifications address the earlier arguments.

D) To defend the previous response

Key Takeaway
Conditions for vision may not be met, and geometric considerations can be used to challenge arguments about perception.

Vision in the Visible vs. Invisible: Quiddity and Conditions
Argument that vision of God differs in quiddity and conditions, with a refinement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the obligation of seeing God in the afterlife does not necessarily follow from the obligation in the visible world, and how a refined argument preserves the necessity of vision without conceding to the Mu'tazila.

Core Idea
Vision's reality may be one, but its conditions differ by the nature of the object seen.

This lesson examines a theological argument about the nature of vision of God in the afterlife. The author first presents a claim that the quiddity of vision differs between visible and invisible, then refines it to better defend the Ash'ari position against the Mu'tazila. You'll see how careful reasoning about quiddity and conditions protects the doctrine of divine vision.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument
Read the passage carefully. Notice that the author first concedes obligation in the visible but questions why it must apply in the invisible.
The argument uses the concept of quiddity (māhiyya) to distinguish the two cases.
2
Identify the two proposals
The passage presents two approaches: one claims quiddity differs, the other suggests accepting unity but permitting different conditions. Distinguish them.
The second approach is presented as 'the first' meaning it is better.
3
Understand the strategic refinement
Ask why the author recommends the second approach. How does it block the Mu'tazila from redefining the dispute?
The Mu'tazila might claim they are only discussing a different type of perception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أنا ( سلمنا الوجوب ) أي وجوب الرؤية ) في الشاهد ( عند حصول تلك الشرائط ( و ) لكنا نقول : (لم يجب ) أي لماذا يجب وجوب الرؤية ( في الغائب ) عند حصولها (إذ ماهية الرؤية في الغائب غير ماهية الرؤية في الشاهد فجاز اختلافهما في اللوازم) والشرائط ( كما يشترط في الشاهد الشروط الستة دون الغائب ) وحينئذ جاز أن تجب رؤية الشاهد عند اجتماعها دون رؤية الغائب . قوله : ( غير ماهية الرؤية في (الشاهد الأولى أن يسلم الاتحاد في الماهية ويجوز اختلاف الشروط بحسب الهويات، إذ على القول بتغاير ماهيتي الرؤيتين يتأتي للمعتزلة أن يقولوا : نزاعنا إنما هو في هذا النوع من الإدراك لا في الرؤية المخالفة لها بالحقية المسماة عندكم بالانكشاف التام وعندنا بالعلم الضروري.
We (accept the obligation) i.e., the obligation of vision (in the visible) upon the occurrence of those conditions (and) but we say: (it is not necessary) i.e., why is the obligation of vision necessary (in the invisible) upon its occurrence? (For the quiddity of vision in the invisible is other than the quiddity of vision in the visible, so it is possible for them to differ in concomitants) and conditions, (just as the six conditions are required in the visible but not in the invisible). Then it is possible that the vision of the visible becomes obligatory upon their meeting without the vision of the invisible. His statement: (other than the quiddity of vision in the visible) it is better to accept unity in quiddity and permit difference of conditions according to identities, because on the view of the two visions differing in quiddity, it becomes possible for the Mu'tazila to say: 'Our dispute is only about this type of perception, not about the vision that differs from it in reality, which you call complete disclosure and we call necessary knowledge.'
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage presents and refines an argument that even if we accept that vision in the visible world requires certain conditions, it does not follow that the same conditions apply to vision of God in the afterlife, because the quiddity (māhiyya) of vision in the invisible is different. The author then comments that it is better to concede unity of quiddity and allow differences in conditions based on the identities of the visible and invisible, to avoid giving the Mu'tazila an opening to redefine the dispute.

Correction: The author shows that while the quiddity may be one, conditions can vary based on the identity of the object, so vision of God may have different requirements.

The Doubt of Direction and Its Refutation
Refuting the doubt that direction is a condition for vision.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the condition of direction for vision is denied when it comes to seeing God.

Core Idea
Divine vision does not require direction because its reality is different.

The doubt of direction argues that vision requires facing the object, which is impossible for God. But the Ash'ari response is to deny that this condition applies to seeing God, either absolutely or because the nature of divine vision is unique. Let's examine the doubt and its refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the doubt of direction
Read the Arabic text describing the doubt: vision requires direction (muqābala) or its equivalent, which is impossible for God.
The doubt is based on ordinary experience.
2
Understand the Ash'ari answer
The answer is to deny the condition of direction. Either absolutely (as the Ash'aris permit vision without facing) or specifically for the invisible because the reality of vision differs.
The key is the difference in reality (ikhtilāf al-ru’yatayn).
3
Reflect on the alternative definition
The passage explains that vision of God is the disclosure of His relation to His essence, not tied to direction or location.
This is based on al-Lubab.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( الثانية : ( من تلك الشبه ( شبهة المقابلة وهي أن شرط الرؤية ) كما علم بالضرورة من التجربة ( المقابلة أو ما في حكمها نحو المرئي في المرآة وإنها أي المقابلة ( مستحيلة في حق الله تعالى لتنزهه عن المكان والجهة والجواب : منع الاشتراط) إما (مطلقاً كما مر من أن الأشاعرة جوزوا رؤية ما لا يكون مقابلا ولا في حكمه بل جوزوا رؤية أعمى الصين [؟] أو في الغائب ) لاختلاف الرؤيتين في الحقيقة، فجاز أن لا يشترط في رؤيته المقابلة المشروطة في رؤية الشاهد وتحقيقه على ما في اللباب أن المراد من الرؤية انكشاف نسبته إلى ذاته المخصوصة كنسبة الانكشاف المسمى بالإبصار إلى سائر المبصرات والانكشاف على وفق المكشوف في الاختصاص بجهة وحيز وفي عدمه. ( الثالثة : ( منها (شبهة الانطباع وهي أن الرؤية انطباع صورة المرئي في الحاسة وهو على الله تعالى محال ) إذ لا يتصور له صورة تنطبع في حاسة، والجواب مثل ما مر ) وهو أن نمنع كون الرؤية بالانطباع إما مطلقاً أو في الغائب لاختلاف الرؤيتين.
(The second: among those doubts is (the doubt of direction, and it is that the condition of vision) as known necessarily from experience (is direction or what is in its ruling, like the visible in the mirror, and it i.e., direction (is impossible with respect to God Almighty due to His transcendence above place and direction. And the answer: denial of the condition) either (absolutely, as previously stated that the Ash'aris permitted vision of what is not facing nor in its ruling, rather they permitted vision of a blind man of China [؟] or in the invisible) due to the difference of the two visions in reality, so it is possible that direction, which is conditioned in vision of the visible, is not conditioned in vision of Him. And its verification according to what is in al-Lubab: the meaning of vision is the disclosure of His relation to His specific essence, like the relation of disclosure called sight to other visible things, and disclosure is in accordance with the disclosed in terms of specification by direction and location and in their absence. (The third: among them (the doubt of impression, and it is that vision is the impression of the image of the visible in the sense organ, and that is impossible with respect to God Almighty) for no image can be conceived to be impressed in a sense organ. And the answer is like what preceded), namely that we deny vision being by impression, either absolutely or in the invisible, due to the difference of the two visions.
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes two rational doubts against the possibility of seeing God. The first is the doubt of direction (muqābala): vision requires facing or something equivalent, which is impossible for God who is beyond space and direction. The answer is to deny the condition, either absolutely (as the Ash'aris hold that vision is possible without facing) or specifically for the invisible, because the reality of vision of God is different. The second doubt is the doubt of impression (intibāʿ): vision is the impression of the image in the sense organ, which is impossible for God. The answer is again to deny that vision is by impression, either absolutely or for the invisible, due to the difference in reality.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Seeing something necessarily requires facing it or being in its direction.
Correction: The Ash'aris hold that vision can occur without facing, especially for God, whose vision is a different kind of disclosure.

Checkpoint

A) Because God is invisible
B) Because the reality of vision differs between the visible and the divine
C) Because direction is not mentioned in the Quran
D) Because the blind man cannot see

Key Takeaway
The condition of direction for vision is denied because divine vision is a disclosure not tied to space.

The Doubt of Impression and Its Refutation
Refuting the doubt that vision requires impression.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the notion of vision as an impression in the sense organ is rejected when applied to God.

Core Idea
Vision of God is not an impression; it is a different kind of disclosure.

The third doubt claims that vision requires the impression of the object's image in the eye, which is impossible for God. The Ash'ari response mirrors the previous one: deny that vision is by impression, either absolutely or for the invisible, because the reality of divine vision differs.

Guided Path
1
Read the doubt of impression
Read the Arabic text: vision is defined as the impression (intibāʿ) of the image in the sense organ, which is impossible for God.
This is a physicalist view of vision.
2
Understand the Ash'ari answer
The answer denies that vision is by impression, either absolutely or specifically for the invisible, due to the difference in reality.
It is the same structure as the previous refutation.
3
Apply the general principle
Recognize that the Ash'ari method is to deny the condition or definition that applies to physical vision when discussing divine vision.
This preserves the possibility of seeing God without physical constraints.

The Criterion for Negating Attributes: Vision Impossible
Definition of the criterion for negation and deduction of vision's impossibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the criterion for negating attributes from God and why vision is impossible according to this criterion.

Core Idea
Absence as praise means presence is deficiency — and that excludes vision of God.

This passage from al-Mawaqif establishes a foundational principle in Islamic theology: any attribute whose absence is praiseworthy must be negated from God. The author applies this to the question of vision (ru'ya), concluding that God cannot be seen. But he also carefully distinguishes attributes from actions, because some actions like pardon or justice are perfections even though they involve 'absence' in a sense. Let's read the original text and unpack its reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation. Let the author's own words set the stage.
The text is a dense argument — read slowly.
2
Identify the criterion
Find the key phrase: 'whatever is among the attributes whose absence is praise and whose presence is deficiency'. This is the logical rule that drives the argument.
3
See the conclusion
The author applies the criterion to vision and concludes that God must be declared transcendent of it — vision is impossible.
The conclusion follows directly from the criterion.
4
Understand the distinction
Notice the clarification: this rule applies to attributes (sifāt), not actions (af‘āl). Actions like pardon and revenge are perfections, so they are not negated by this criterion.
This prevents misunderstanding the scope of the rule.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الخامس - المقصد الأول : في الرؤية والكلام. وما كان من الصفات عدمه مدحاً كان وجوده نقصاً، الله يجب تنزيه عنه، فظهر أنه يمتنع رؤيته، وإنما قلنا من الصفات احترازا عن الأفعال كالعفو والانتقام، فإن الأول فضل والثاني عدل وكلاهما كمال.
The fifth observation - the first objective: on vision and speech. And whatever is among the attributes whose absence is praise and whose presence is deficiency, God must be declared transcendent of it, so it appears that His vision is impossible. And we only said 'from attributes' as a precaution against actions like pardon and revenge, for the first is grace and the second is justice, and both are perfection.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage begins by stating the topic: the fifth observation on vision and speech. It then presents a criterion: any attribute such that its absence is considered praise and its presence is considered deficiency must be negated from God. From this, it concludes that vision of God is impossible, because being seen would be a deficiency. The author then clarifies that they are referring specifically to attributes, not actions, because actions like pardon (which is grace) and revenge (which is justice) are perfections even though they are acts. This distinction is important for the subsequent arguments.

Key Terms
الصفات
الأفعال
الرؤية
المدح
النقص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that any attribute whose absence is praised is automatically negated from God, including actions like forgiveness.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the criterion applies only to attributes (sifāt), not to actions (af‘āl); actions like pardon and revenge are perfections, so their absence is not necessarily praise.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the negation of vision argued for God?
A) Because vision is an action
B) Because absence of vision is praise and presence would be deficiency
C) Because God cannot be seen in any sense
D) Because the Quran says so

Key Takeaway
The criterion that absence is praise and presence is deficiency is used to negate vision from God, but it applies only to attributes, not actions.

Distinguishing Idrak from Absolute Vision
The author refutes the argument by distinguishing between 'idrak' (comprehensive perception) and absolute vision.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Iji distinguishes between comprehensive perception (idrak) and absolute vision to defend the possibility of seeing Allah.

Core Idea
Negating comprehensive perception does not negate absolute vision.

In this passage, al-Iji responds to an objection that the verse 'Sight does not perceive Him' proves the impossibility of seeing Allah. He argues that the key is to understand what 'idrak' means. By carefully defining the term and showing its root meaning, he demonstrates that the verse only denies a specific kind of vision, not all vision.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Pay attention to how al-Iji defines 'idrak' and the example he gives.
2
Identify the definition of idrak
Al-Iji says idrak is 'vision in the manner of encompassing the sides of the visible object'. Underline this definition.
Note the root meaning of 'idrak' as attainment or reaching.
3
Examine the linguistic evidence
The author gives three examples of the root 'idrak': fruit reaching ripeness, a boy reaching puberty, and the general sense of attaining. Understand how these support the transfer to 'encompassing vision'.
The examples show that idrak implies full attainment, hence encompassing.
4
Understand the distinction and objection
Al-Iji says negating encompassing vision does not negate absolute vision. The objector claims you cannot negate one while affirming the other. The author refutes with the example: 'I saw him but my sight did not perceive him.'
This example shows that the reverse (affirming vision while denying idrak) is possible.
5
Reflect on the significance
This distinction allows Muslims to affirm that Allah can be seen (in the afterlife) while still respecting the verse that denies comprehensive perception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب : أما عن الوجه الأول في الاستدلال بالآية فمن وجوه : الأول : إن الإدراك هو الرؤية على نعت الإحاطة بجوانب المرئي، إذ حقيقته النيل والوصول وإنا لمدركون أي ملحقون وأدركت الثمرة أي وصلت إلى حد النضج، وأدرك الغلام أي بلغ ثم نقل إلى الرؤية المحيطة لكونها أقرب إلى تلك الحقيقة والرؤية المتكيفة بكيفية الإحاطة أخص مطلقا من الرؤية المطلقة فلا يلزم من نفيها أي نفي المحيطة عن الباري سبحانه وتعالى لامتناع الإحاطة نفيها أي نفي المطلقة عنه. قوله : لا يصح نفي أحدهما مع إثبات أخر . قلنا : ممنوع بل يصح أن يقال : رأيته وما أدركه بصري أي لم يحط به من جوانبه وإن لم يصح عكسه.
The answer: As for the first aspect of the proof from the verse, it has several directions: First: Indeed, 'idrak' (perception) is vision in the manner of encompassing (ihata) the sides of the visible object, for its essence is attainment and reaching. We say 'mudrikun' (perceiving) meaning reaching. 'I adraktu al-thamara' means the fruit reached ripeness. 'Adraka al-ghulam' means the boy reached puberty. Then it was transferred to 'encompassing vision' because it is closer to that reality. And vision qualified with the quality of encompassment is absolutely more specific than absolute vision. So negating it, i.e., negating encompassing vision from God because encompassment is impossible, does not necessitate negating absolute vision from Him. He said: 'It is not valid to negate one while affirming the other.' We say: It is not accepted; rather, it is valid to say: 'I saw him but my sight did not perceive him,' meaning it did not encompass him from his sides, though the reverse is not valid.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage gives the first response to the argument that the verse 'Sight does not perceive Him' proves impossibility of vision. The author distinguishes between 'idrak' (comprehensive perception) and mere vision. Idrak is defined as vision that fully encompasses the object. The Arabic root indicates attainment and reaching. Since God cannot be encompassed, negating idrak does not negate vision itself. The objector's claim that negation of one implies negation of the other is rejected with the example: one can see something without fully perceiving (encompassing) it.

Third and Fourth Responses: Temporal and Modal Limitations
The author gives two responses limiting the negation of vision in the verse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two key responses that limit the scope of the verse negating vision of Allah to either temporal or modal contexts.

Core Idea
The verse 'sights do not see Him' may be limited in time or kind.

In this passage, the author presents two additional arguments to show that the verse does not necessarily negate the possibility of seeing Allah in the afterlife. The third response argues that the negation may be limited to this world, while the fourth specifies that it may refer to physical vision with conditions. These responses help reconcile the verse with the belief in the beatific vision.

Guided Path
1
Read the third response
Focus on the distinction between an absolute negative and a perpetual negative. The author says the verse is an absolute negative, not applying to all times.
Absolute negative negates existence at a time, perpetual negates at all times.
2
Read the fourth response
Notice how the author distinguishes between negation of vision by a bodily organ with facing and impression, and vision in a different manner.
This preserves the possibility of a non-physical vision of Allah.
3
Consider the overall argument
Reflect on how these two responses together show that the verse does not definitively negate vision in the afterlife.
This is part of a larger debate in kalam.

الثالث : من تلك الوجوه أنها أي الآية وإن عمت في الأشخاص باستغراق اللام فإنها لا تعم في الأزمان فإنها سالبة مطلقة لا دائمة ونحن نقول بموجبه حيث لا يرى في الدنيا. الرابع : منها أن الآية تدل على أن الأبصار لا تراه ولا يلزم منه أن المبصرين لا يرونه لجواز أن يكون ذلك النفي المذكور في الآية نفياً للرؤية بالجارحة مواجهة وانطباعاً كما هو العادة فلا يلزم نفي الرؤية بالجارحة مطلقاً.
Third of those directions: that the verse, even if it is universal with respect to persons by the generality of the lam, it is not universal with respect to times, for it is an absolute negative, not a perpetual negative. And we affirm it where He is not seen in this world. Fourth: that the verse indicates that 'sights do not see Him' and it does not necessitate that seers do not see Him, because it is possible that the negation mentioned in the verse is a negation of vision by the organ (al-jariha) with facing and impression as is customary, so the negation of vision by the organ absolutely is not necessitated.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage gives two more responses. The third: even if the verse applies to all persons (all eyes), it may not apply to all times. It could be an absolute negative (salb mutlaq) rather than a perpetual negative (salb da'im). So it only negates vision in this world, not in the afterlife. The fourth: the negation may be specifically about physical vision by the bodily eye involving facing and impression, which is how vision normally occurs. This does not negate a different kind of vision, such as the vision of God in the afterlife without those conditions.

Response to the Second Argument: Praise Does Not Prove Impossibility
The author rejects that praise implies impossibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the opponent's claim that the verse is a praise of God does not establish the impossibility of seeing God.

Core Idea
Praise for negating impossibility still makes sense.

The opponent argues that the verse is a praise of God for not being seen, implying that vision must be possible for the praise to be meaningful. Imam al-Sanusi refutes this by first challenging the claim that it is praise, and then showing that even if it were, impossibility does not prevent praise. This lesson walks through that rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: the author quotes the opponent's claim, then responds layer by layer.
The passage contains internal dialogue marked by 'قوله'.
2
Identify the opponent's claim and the first response
The opponent says the verse is a praise for not being seen. The author responds: 'This is your claim, so where is the proof?' and argues that even if it is praise, it does not prove impossibility.
The author flips the logic: if the praise were meaningful only if vision were possible, then it would actually prove possibility.
3
Understand the distinction between visibility and vision
The commentary clarifies that the praise is for negating visibility (being seen by sight), not for negating vision itself. This distinction was already made in the first answer.
This is a subtle but crucial point in the broader debate.
4
Examine the final counterexample
The author concludes that impossibility does not prevent praise, using Quranic examples of praise for negating a partner and a son, both impossible for God.
This dismantles the opponent's assumption that praise implies possibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما عن الوجه الثاني : أي وأما الجواب عن الوجه الثاني من وجهي الاستدلال بالآية وهو قوله تمدح الباري بأنه لا يرى فنقول هذا مدعاكم فأين الدليل عليه وإذا ثبت أن سياق الكلام يقتضي أنه تمدح لم يكن لكم فيه دليل على امتناع رؤيته بل لنافيه الحجة على صحة الرؤية لأنه لو امتنعت رؤيته لما حصل المدح بنفيها عنه إذ لا مدح للمعدوم بأنه لا يرى حيث لم يكن له ذلك وإنما المدح فيه أي في عدم الرؤية للممتنع المتعزز بحجاب الكبرياء.

قوله : فأين الدليل عليه فإن قلت قد أشار الشارح إلى دليله وقد ذكرت وجه الاستدلال به قلت ذلك الدليل إنما يدل على التمدح بنفي المبصرية لا بنفي في الرؤية والفرق قد سبق في الجواب الأول. قوله : بأنه لو امتنعت رؤيته لما حصل المدح فيه نظر إذ الحق أن امتناع الشيء لا يمنع التمدح بنفيه كيف وقد ورد في القرآن العزيز التمدح بنفي الشريك ونفي اتخاذ الولد مع امتناعهما في حقه تعالى.
As for the second aspect: it is his statement that God praises Himself by not being seen. We say: This is your claim, so where is the proof for it? And if it is established that the context requires that it is praise, there is no proof in it for the impossibility of seeing Him. Rather, for the one who negates, there is proof for the validity of vision, because if vision were impossible, then praise for negating it would not be obtained, for there is no praise for a non-existent thing that it is not seen, since it does not have that. Rather, praise for it (i.e., for the absence of vision) is for something that is impossible but dignified by the veil of majesty.

He said: 'So where is the proof?' If you say: The commentator has pointed to his proof and I have mentioned the aspect of inference from it. I say: That proof only indicates praise for negating visibility (al-mubsariyya), not for negating vision, and the difference has already preceded in the first answer. He said: 'If vision were impossible, then praise would not be obtained' — there is consideration in it, for the truth is that impossibility of a thing does not prevent praise for negating it, as in the Mighty Quran praise for negating a partner and negating taking a son, despite their impossibility in His exalted right.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage responds to the second argument that the verse is a praise of God for not being seen. The author argues that this claim is unproven; the context does not necessarily make it a praise. Furthermore, even if it were praise, it does not prove impossibility. The opponent's reasoning that praise would be meaningless if vision were possible is invalid, because God also praises Himself for things that are impossible, such as having no partner or son. So praise for negating vision does not require that vision be possible in itself. The commentary also clarifies that the praise is for negating visibility (being seen by sight) not for negating absolute vision.

Correction: The author shows that praise can be given for negating impossible things, as in the Quranic praises for having no partner or son.

The First Proof: God's Essence Unknown to Humans
Al-Iji presents the first proof that God's essence is unknown to humans.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Iji's first proof that God's essence is unknown to humans, including the three categories of what we can know about Him.

Core Idea
We know God's attributes, not His essence.

Al-Iji opens the discussion on whether we can know God's very essence (ḥaqīqa). Most verifying scholars hold that in this world, we cannot. The first proof he offers is that everything we know of God falls into three types: universal accidents, negations, or relations—all additional to the essence itself. This lesson unpacks that proof and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim and its supporters
Read the passage to find the main claim: that God's reality is unknown to humans, and who holds this view.
Notice that it's attributed to the majority of verifiers.
2
Examine the first proof's structure
Identify the three categories of what we know about God: universal accidents (like existence), negations (like being eternal), and relations (like being creator).
These are all additional to the essence.
3
Understand the objection and response
The commentator raises an objection: you cannot assume all humans only know these things. Reflect on why this objection might not disprove the proof.
The proof is logical, not empirical.
4
Connect to the broader topic
Consider why this distinction matters: if essence were known, then attributes would be part of essence, leading to anthropomorphism or other errors.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ المقصد الثاني : في العلم بحقيقة الله ] في العلم بحقيقة الله والكلام في الوقوع والجواز وفيه مقامان : المقام الأول : الوقوع أن حقيقة الله تعالى غير معلومة للبشر وعليه جمهور المحققين من الفرق الإسلامية وغيرهم وقد خالف فيه كثير من المتكلمين من أصحابنا والمعتزلة لنا وجهان : الأول : إن المعلوم منه أعراض عامة كالوجود أو سلوب ككونه واجباً لا يقبل العدم أزلياً لا يسبقه عدم أبدياً لا يلحقه عدم ليس بجوهر ولا في مكان أو إضافات ككونه خالقاً قادراً عالماً فإن هذه الصفات كلها إضافات لأن الإضافة تطلق على النسبة المتكررة وعلى معروضها قال الآمدي كل ما ندركه منه صفات خارجة عن
قوله : ( غير معلومة للبشر أي في الدنيا وهل يعلم في الجنة بعد رؤيته فيه تردد .
قوله : ( الأول المعلوم منه أعراض عامة إلخ ) يرد عليه أنا لا نسلم أن معلوم كل أحد ما ذكرتم ومن أين لكم الإحاطة بأفراد البشر ومعلوماتهم.
قوله : ( لأن الإضافة تطلق على النسبة المتكررة وعلى معروضها فالأولى هي الحقيقي والثانية المشهوري كما سبق وكونه تعالى خالقاً وعالماً مثلاً من الأول لأن الخالقية بالنسبة إلى المخلوقية وبالعكس وكذا العالمية، فقول الشارح فإن هذه الصفات إشارة إلى كونه خالقاً وكونه قادراً وكونه عالماً كما الظاهر من المتن والمقصود من التعليل أعني قوله : لأن الإضافة إلخ هو التنبيه على أنها لا تختص بمعروض النسبة المتكررة حتى لا يصلح التمثيل بنفس هو
[The Second Purpose: On Knowledge of the Reality of God] On knowledge of the reality of God, and the discussion of its occurrence and possibility, and it contains two stations. The first station: the occurrence: that the reality of God Most High is unknown to humans, and upon this is the majority of verifiers from the Islamic schools and others. Many theologians from our companions and the Mu'tazila have opposed this. We have two proofs. The first: what is known of Him are universal accidents like existence, or negations like that He is necessary—He does not accept nonexistence—eternal—no non-existence precedes Him—everlasting—no non-existence follows Him—He is not a substance nor in a place—or relations like that He is creator, powerful, knowing. For these attributes are all relations, because 'relation' (al-idafa) is applied to the repeated proportion and to its correlative. Al-Amidi said: 'Everything we perceive of Him are attributes extrinsic to...' He said: (unknown to humans) meaning in this world, and there is hesitation whether it is known in Paradise after seeing Him. He said: (the first: what is known of Him are universal accidents, etc.) It is objected: we do not grant that what everyone knows is what you mentioned; from where do you have comprehensive knowledge of all individuals and their knowledge? He said: (because al-idafa is applied to the repeated proportion and to its correlative, so the first is the real and the second is the common, as previously. And that He Most High is creator and knowing, for example, is of the first, because creatorship is relative to being created and vice versa, and likewise knowledge. So the commentator's statement that 'these attributes' refers to His being creator, powerful, knowing, as is apparent from the text, and the intent of the explanation (his statement 'because al-idafa etc.') is to point out that they are not restricted to the correlative of the repeated proportion, so that the example is not valid by itself.)
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage opens the second main section (al-maqṣad al-thānī) on the knowledge of God's essence (ḥaqīqa). It first states the issue: is it possible to know God's essence? It then presents the first position: that God's essence is unknown to humans in this world, which is the view of most verifying scholars. Many theologians from the Ash'aris and Mu'tazila disagree. The first proof (al-wajh al-awwal) is given: what humans know of God are either universal accidents (like existence), negations (like being eternal, not a substance), or relational attributes (like being creator, powerful, knowing). All these are additional to the essence. The commentary by the sharh explains that 'unknown to humans' applies to this world, with some doubt about knowledge in Paradise after vision. Then a potential objection is raised: not everyone's knowledge is limited to what you listed; you cannot survey all human knowledge. Finally, the commentator explains the term 'al-idāfa' (relation) and argues that the attributes mentioned are relational and do not pertain to the essence itself.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That knowing God's attributes (like being Creator) directly informs us of His essence.
Correction: Al-Iji argues that all we know are external attributes (accidents, negations, relations), which are additional to the essence, not the essence itself.

B) Only His existence

Key Takeaway
Al-Iji's first proof establishes that human knowledge of God is limited to attributes that are not His essence, affirming divine transcendence.

Knowledge of Attributes Does Not Reveal the Specific Essence
The commentator argues that attributes do not yield knowledge of the specific essence, with a magnet analogy and response to an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why knowledge of God's attributes does not provide knowledge of His specific essence, and how the magnet analogy illustrates this distinction.

Core Idea
Attributes point to a distinct essence but do not unveil its inner reality.

This lesson examines a key principle in Islamic theology: that knowing God's attributes—such as His power, knowledge, and being Creator—does not give us direct knowledge of His specific essence. The commentator uses the example of a magnet to illustrate this distinction, and then addresses a potential objection from those who equate existence with essence. By the end, you'll see why attributes serve as indicators of a distinct reality without revealing its intrinsic nature.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the logical flow from listing attributes to the main claim and analogy.
2
Identify the three attribute categories
The passage mentions essential, relational, and negative attributes. Find each type in the text.
Essential attributes are those like knowledge and power; relational are like being Creator; negative are not listed explicitly but implied.
3
Understand the main claim
The central thesis: knowledge of attributes does not necessitate knowledge of the specific essence. It only indicates that there is a distinct essence.
This is a crucial distinction between indication and full knowledge.
4
Analyze the magnet analogy
The magnet analogy shows that observing an effect (attracting iron) does not give knowledge of the magnet's inner reality, only that it has a specific reality different from others.
This helps to concretize the abstract distinction.
5
Examine the objection and response
The objection claims that if existence is identical to essence, knowing existence (through attributes) would be knowing essence. The response distinguishes between tasdiq (assent) and tasawwur (conception).
Assent that something exists is not the same as conceptualizing its quiddity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
علمنا ذاته كصفات النفس من العلم والقدرة وغيرهما والصفات الإضافية كونه خالقاً ومبدأ والصفات السلبية. ولا شك أن العلم بهذه الصفات لا يوجب العلم بالحقيقة المخصوصة ما هي في حد ذاتها، بل تدل هذه الصفات على أن ثمة حقيقة مخصوصة متميزة في نفسها عن سائر الحقائق، وأما عين تلك الحقيقة الموصوفة عليها فلا تدل هي بخصوصيتها، كما لا يلزم من صدور الأثر الخاص أعني جذب الحديد عن المغناطيس العلم بحقيقته المعينة، بل بأن حقيقته حقيقة مخصوصة مغايرة لسائر الحقائق ممتازة عنها في حد نفسها. قوله: لا يوجب العلم بالحقيقة المخصوصة. لا يقال: الوجود عين الذات عند كثير من المحققين فالعلم به علم بها، لأنا نقول: معنى العلم بالوجود التصديق بأنه موجود وهذا لا يستدعي تصور وجوده الخاص بحقيقته، وكذا الكلام في سائر الصفات.
We know His essence through attributes of the self such as knowledge and power and others, and the relational attributes such as being Creator and Originator, and the negative attributes. There is no doubt that knowledge of these attributes does not necessitate knowledge of the specific essence of what it is in itself, rather these attributes indicate that there is a specific essence distinct in itself from other essences. As for the very essence described by them, it does not indicate its specificity, just as the occurrence of the specific effect, I mean the attraction of iron by a magnet, does not necessitate knowledge of its specific reality, but rather that its reality is a specific reality different from other realities distinguished from them in its own boundary. His saying: it does not necessitate knowledge of the specific essence. It is not said: Existence is identical to the essence according to many verifiers, so knowledge of it is knowledge of it. For we say: The meaning of knowledge of existence is the assent that it exists, and this does not require the conception of its specific existence by its reality, and similarly in the case of other attributes.
Source pages p. 161

Commentary
This passage presents a definitional argument that knowledge of God's attributes does not yield knowledge of His specific essence. It begins by listing types of attributes: essential (like knowledge and power), relational (like Creator), and negative. The key claim is that knowing these attributes only informs us that there is a distinct essence, not what that essence is in itself. The magnet analogy illustrates this: knowing that a magnet attracts iron does not reveal the inner reality of magnetism. Then an objection is raised: if essence is identical to existence (as some theologians hold), then knowing existence should be knowing essence. The answer clarifies that knowledge of existence is merely an assent (tasdiq) that the thing exists, not a conception (tasawwur) of its specific quiddity. The same holds for all attributes.

Correction: Attributes only indicate the existence of a distinct essence, not its inner reality.

Will you from now on focus more on affirming His existence (tasdiq) rather than pretending to conceive Him?
?
The magnet analogy shows that effects do not reveal the inner reality of their cause. In your daily life, do you sometimes act as if you fully know God because you see His effects? How can you cultivate a faith that rests on humility before the unknown essence rather than on the illusion of full knowledge? Commit to one practical step to remind yourself of this limit.
?
If knowledge of existence is assent, not conception, then your faith is not about having God figured out. Are you comfortable with that? Or do you secretly wish for a conceptual grasp? Examine your heart: is your trust in God based on what you know about Him or on the fact that He is real even though you cannot conceive Him? Which is more sincere?

Why Known Attributes Don't Reveal God's Unique Essence
Argument: known attributes are shareable; objection and answer about tawheed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why knowing God's attributes (like existence, knowledge, power) does not automatically identify His unique essence, and why the argument for tawheed remains necessary despite knowing those attributes.

Core Idea
Shared attributes point to a common concept, not to God's unique essence.

This passage presents a crucial objection and response within the discourse on divine attributes. The commentator argues that the attributes we know of God—existence, knowledge, power, etc.—are universal concepts that can apply to many beings. Therefore, simply knowing these attributes does not give us knowledge of God's specific essence; we still need to negate any partner (tawheed). An objector says that tawheed itself is known by proof, so why argue? The answer lies in distinguishing between the attributes and the specific essence.

Guided Path
1
Understand the claim: attributes are shareable
Read the first part of the passage. Notice that attributes like existence, knowledge, etc., are universal—they can be conceived as applying to multiple beings. This is the core of the argument.
Think of 'existent'—many things exist, not just God.
2
Identify the objection about tawheed being already known
The objector points out that we already know God is one (tawheed) by definitive proofs. So if we already know He is unique, why do we need an argument that attributes are shareable?
The objector thinks the argument is redundant.
3
Grasp the answer: the specific essence is not known through attributes
The commentator answers that the specific essence itself prevents sharing (like any individual), but that essence is not what we know from these attributes. What we know are universal concepts; the specific essence remains unknown and is what we seek to affirm through tawheed.
The argument for tawheed is needed to reach the specific essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني: إن كل ما يعلم منه من كونه موجوداً وعالماً وقادراً ومريداً وخالقاً إلى غير ذلك لا يمنع تصوره الشركة فيه، ولذلك يحتاج في نفيه. قوله: (لا يمنع تصوره الشركة فيه ولذلك يحتاج إلخ) اعترض عليه بأن من جملة ما علم منه الوحدانية بأدلتها القاطعة، ومع اعتبار ذلك لا يتصور الشركة ولا الافتقار إلى بيان التوحيد. والجواب: أن ذاته المخصوصة يمنع تصوره من الشركة لأن الموجودات الشخصية كذلك، فليس المعلوم ذاته المخصوصة، وعكسه أعني قولنا: ليس ذاته المخصوصة بالمعلوم هو المطلوب.
The second: All that is known of Him, such as His being existent, knowing, powerful, willing, creator, and so on, does not prevent its conception from being shared (by others), therefore there is need to negate [sharing]. His saying: 'Its conception does not prevent sharing in it, and therefore there is need etc.' an objection is raised against it: among what is known of Him is oneness (tawheed) by its definitive proofs, and with that consideration, sharing is inconceivable nor is there need for the demonstration of tawheed. The answer: That His specific essence prevents its conception from sharing, because individual existents are like that. So what is known is not His specific essence, and its opposite, meaning our statement: 'His specific essence is not known' is what is sought.
Source pages p. 161

Commentary
This passage presents a second argument: the attributes known of God (existence, knowledge, power, etc.) are universal concepts that can be shared by multiple beings (e.g., existence is common to all existents). Therefore, knowing these attributes does not uniquely identify God's essence; we need to negate sharing (tawheed) to specify Him. An objection counters that God's oneness (tawheed) is also known by definitive proofs, and if we already know He is one, then sharing is impossible and the argument is redundant. The answer distinguishes: the specific essence itself, like any individual, cannot be shared because individuality precludes sharing. What we know via attributes is not the specific essence; therefore the claim that the specific essence is not known stands as desired.

Correction: Tawheed is an attribute known by proof, but it does not reveal the inner reality of the essence. The specific essence remains unknown through attributes; tawheed is needed to affirm uniqueness of that essence.

The Ash'ari Doctrine of Acquisition
Original text stating Ash'ari doctrine of acquisition (kash).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash‘ari doctrine of acquisition (kash) and how it reconciles divine omnipotence with human responsibility.

Core Idea
The servant acquires his act without causing it — only God creates.

The Ash‘ari school is known for its distinctive doctrine of acquisition (kash), which seeks to affirm both God's absolute power over all things and the human sense of responsibility for voluntary actions. In this passage from the matn, the author lays out the core position: voluntary acts occur solely by God's power, yet the servant's act is 'acquired' in a specific sense. Let's read carefully to see how this is defined.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage for overall meaning
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the gist. Notice the key terms: قدرة الله, كسب, محل.
The passage is short but dense.
2
Identify the core assertion
Find the sentence that states where the power for the act lies. The passage begins with a clear declaration.
It says 'by the power of God alone'.
3
Understand the definition of kash
After stating that the act is created by God, it defines what the servant's acquisition is. Look for the phrase 'المراد بكسبه'.
Acquisition is concurrence without effect.
4
Reflect on the metaphysical implications
Consider how this doctrine answers the problem of human free will vs. divine predestination. What does it affirm and deny?
The servant is a locus (محل) but not a cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في أن أفعال العباد الاختيارية واقعة بقدرة الله سبحانه وتعالى وحدها) ليس لقدرتهم تأثير فيها بل الله سبحانه أجرى عادته بأ يوجد في العبد قدرة واختياراً فإ لم يكن هناك مانع أوجد فيه فعله المقدور مقارناً لهما، فيكون فعل العبد مخلوقاً لله إبداعاً وإحداثاً ومكسوباً للعبد ، والمراد بكسبه إياه مقارنته لقدرته وإرادته من غير أن يكون هناك منه تأثير أو مدخل في وجوده سوی کونه ،محلا له، وهذا مذهب الشيخ الحسن الأشعري
That the voluntary acts of servants occur by the power of God alone, their power having no effect in them; rather God has followed His custom that He creates in the servant a power and choice; then if there is no obstacle, He creates in him his intended act concurrent with them. So the servant's act is created by God in terms of origination and bringing into existence, and is an acquisition of the servant. The meaning of his acquiring it is its being concurrent with his power and will, without there being from him any effect or entry into its existence except his being a locus for it. And this is the doctrine of Shaykh al-Hasan al-Ash‘ari.
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal statement from the original text (matn) defining the Ash‘ari position on human acts. It begins by asserting that all voluntary acts of servants occur solely by God's power—the servant's own power has no effect in bringing the act into existence. However, God's custom (jari ‘adatuhu) is to create in the servant a power and choice. When no obstacle prevents the act, God creates the act concurrently with that power and choice. The act is thus 'created' (makhluq) by God in terms of origin (ibda‘) and bringing into existence (ihdath), while being 'acquired' (maksub) by the servant. Acquisition (kash) means the act's concurrence with the servant's power and will without any causal contribution from the servant—the servant is merely a locus (mahall) for the act. The passage concludes by attributing this view to al-Ash‘ari.

قدرة الله
كسب
محل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'acquisition' implies the servant creates his act.
Correction: Here it is clearly defined as mere concurrence with the servant's power and will, without any causal effect.

Checkpoint

A) The servant creates the act independently.
B) The servant's power produces the act with divine assistance.
C) The act is concurrent with the servant's power and will without any causal effect from the servant.
D) The servant chooses the act but God creates it separately.

Key Takeaway
The Ash‘ari doctrine teaches that God alone creates all acts, but the servant acquires his act through concurrence of power and will without causal contribution.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that every action you perform is created solely by God and you have no causal power, how does that change the way you pray for strength to avoid sin and to do good? Will you rely more on God's creation of your acts or on your own resolve?
?
Consider a sin you struggle with. In the Ash'ari view, God creates that act in you while you merely acquire it. Does that make you less responsible, or does it deepen your sense of need for God's grace to create obedience in you? How should you respond to your sin in light of this?
?
The passage says God 'follows His custom' of creating power and choice in you before your acts. Have you ever felt that your choices are real and weighty? How can you reconcile that feeling with the doctrine that your choices are created and have no causal effect? Does that realization humble you or unsettle you?

The Three Positions on Divine and Human Power
Presents three views on the involvement of divine and human power in action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three classical positions on how divine power and human power participate in a single action, as presented by al-Ustadh, al-Qadi, and the contrast between Mu'tazila and philosophers.

Core Idea
Three schools, three ways to divide the act between God and servant.

This lesson introduces the three major views on the relationship of divine and human power in the occurrence of an act. The Ash'ari theologian al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini, al-Qadi al-Baqillani, and the philosophers/Mu'tazila each offer a distinct account. The passage sets out these positions with an illustrative example.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice the structure: first the topic, then the three views.
The Arabic is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the three views
Locate the statements of al-Ustadh, al-Qadi, and the Mu'tazila/philosophers. What does each one attribute to divine and human power?
Al-Ustadh allows two influences on one effect.
3
Study the example
The slapping of an orphan illustrates al-Qadi's view: the essence of the act comes from God, the moral quality from the servant.
Consider why this example was chosen.
4
Reflect on the differences
Think about the implications of each view for human responsibility and divine omnipotence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد السادس - المقصد الأول : في أفعاله تعالى وأفعال العباد معاً ثم اختلفوا (فقال الأستاذ بمجموع القدرتين على أن يتعلقا جميعاً بالفعل) نفسه، وجوز اجتماع المؤثرين على أثر واحد ( وقال القاضي : على أن تتعلق قدرة الله بأصل الفعل وقدرة العبد ) بصفته أعني بكونه طاعة ومعصية) إلى غير ذلك من الأوصاف التي لا توصف بها أفعاله تعالى ( كما في لطم اليتيم تأديباً أو إيذاء) فإن ذات اللطم واقعة بقدرة الله وتأثيره وكونه طاعة على الأول ومعصية على الثاني بقدرة بأن وقوع الفعل عند المعتزلة على سبيل الاختيار، وعند الحكماء على سبيل الوجوب لكنه غير تام لأن عدم الإيجاب، إنما هو بالنسبة إلى نفس القدرة. وأما مع تمام الشرائط من الإرادة وغيرها فليس إلا الوجوب، اللهم إلا أن يقال : مذهب المعتزلة أن صدور الفعل عن المختار ولو بعد تمام الشرائط على سبيل الصحة دون الوجوب بناء على كفاية الرجحان في الوقوع وإن كان مردوداً عند المحققين كما سبق، وهاهنا احتمالان آخران أحدهما أن يقال : ذات القدرة لا توجب الفعل عند المعتزلة إنما الإيجاب من التعلق الذي يجوز أن يكون بدله تعلق آخر وعند الحكيم ذات القدرة توجب التعلق المخصوص الموجب للفعل بأن تكون القدرة المؤثرة عندهم مع الفعل، كما أن القدرة الكاسبة عندنا كذلك والاحتمال الثاني أن يريد الحكماء بتعلق قدرة الله تعالى بقدرة العبد تعلقها بها من حيث تأثيرها في الفعل حتى تكون قدرة العبد كالآلة لقدرة الله تعالى وهي توجب الفعل بواسطتها، وعلى هذا يحصل الفرق بين مذهب الحكماء والمعتزلة ويندفع الاعتراض على القاضي بأنه لا معنى لنفي كون قدرة العبد متعلقة بقدرة الله تعالى لكنه خلاف ما اشتهر عن من إثبات القدرة المؤثرة للعبد بالنسبة إلى فعله الاختياري كما يفهم من الضابطة المذكورة أيضاً ويدل عليه تنصيص الآمدي فى أبكار الأفكار على أن فعل العبد عند إمام الحرمين واقع بقدرته مع تصريح المصنف بأنه موافق للحكماء في المذهب فالأظهر أن الفرق بين المذهبين إنما هو باعتبار أن خلق الله تعالى قدرة العبد بلا واسطة وبالاختيار وعند الفلاسفة بها الحكماء وبالإيجاب .
The Sixth Observatory - First Purpose: On His acts, may He be exalted, and the acts of servants together. Then they differed: al-Ustadh said that the two powers together both attach to the act itself, and he permitted the conjunction of two influences on one effect. Al-Qadi said that God's power attaches to the essence of the act and the servant's power to its quality, namely being obedience or disobedience, and other attributes that cannot be attributed to His acts, as in slapping the orphan as discipline or as injury: the essence of the slap occurs by God's power and His influence, and its being obedience in the first case and disobedience in the second case by the servant's power. [He said] that for the Mu'tazila the occurrence of the act is by way of choice, and for the philosophers by way of necessity, but this is not complete because the absence of necessitation is only relative to the power itself. But with the completion of conditions, such as will and others, there is nothing but necessity. Unless it is said: the Mu'tazila hold that the act proceeds from the free agent even after the completion of conditions as permissible without necessity, based on the sufficiency of preponderance for occurrence, though rejected by the verifiers as has preceded. Here there are two other possibilities: one is to say that the essence of power does not necessitate the act for the Mu'tazila; rather necessitation comes from the attachment which may be replaced by another attachment. For the philosopher, the essence of power necessitates the specific attachment that necessitates the act, since for them the influencing power is with the act, just as for us the acquisitive power is. The second possibility is that the philosophers intend by the attachment of God's power to the servant's power its attachment to it in terms of its influence in the act, so that the servant's power becomes like an instrument for God's power, and it necessitates the act through it. On this, the difference between the school of the philosophers and the Mu'tazila is obtained, and the objection against al-Qadi is repelled, that there is no meaning to denying that the servant's power is attached to God's power. But it contradicts what is known from him of affirming the servant's influencing power regarding his voluntary act, as is understood from the aforementioned rule. This is also indicated by al-Amidi's specification in Abkar al-Afkar that the servant's act, according to Imam al-Haramayn, occurs by his power, along with the author's statement that he agrees with the philosophers in doctrine. So the apparent view is that the difference between the two schools is only in that God's creation of the servant's power is without intermediary and by choice, whereas for the philosophers it is by necessity.
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage presents the three main positions on how divine and human power relate to a single action. It begins by stating the topic: God's acts and human acts together. Then it reports three views: (1) al-Ustadh (Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini) who holds that both powers together attach to the act itself, allowing two influences on one effect. (2) Al-Qadi (al-Baqillani) who holds that God's power attaches to the essence of the act and human power attaches to its quality (obedience/disobedience). (3) The philosophers (and, as will appear later, Imam al-Haramayn) who hold that the act occurs necessarily when conditions are met. The passage then expands on the distinction between Mu'tazila (choice) and philosophers (necessity). It notes that the difference is not absolute: for the Mu'tazila, after all conditions are complete, the act is still without necessity (only permissible), while for philosophers it becomes necessary. Two further possibilities are explored to reconcile the schools: first, that for Mu'tazila the power does not necessitate the act but rather the attachment can change; second, that the philosophers see the servant's power as instrumental to God's power. The passage concludes that the clearest difference is that God creates human power directly and by choice (Mu'tazila) or by necessity (philosophers). The author also notes a conflict in ascribing to al-Qadi a denial of the servant's power being attached to God's power, and refers to al-Amidi's testimony that Imam al-Haramayn actually agrees with the philosophers.

القدرة الحادثة
المؤثر
التعلق
الاختيار
الوجوب

Checkpoint

A) Mu'tazila say the act occurs by necessity, philosophers by choice
B) Mu'tazila say the act occurs by choice, philosophers by necessity
C) Mu'tazila deny God's power, philosophers affirm it

Key Takeaway
The three views offer different models for how divine omnipotence and human responsibility coexist in a single action.
